        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liBXBvUkgbqQA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1016/j.cossms.2012.04.002"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ellis, B.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium and sodium-ion energy storage batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ellis, Brian L.; Nazar, Linda F.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Current Opinion in Solid State &amp; Materials Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Owing to almost unmatched volumetric energy d., Li-ion batteries have dominated the portable electronics industry and solid state electrochem. literature for the past 20 years.  Not only will that continue, but they are also now powering plug-in hybrid elec. vehicles and elec. vehicles.  In light of possible concerns over rising lithium costs in the future, Na and Na-ion batteries have re-emerged as candidates for medium and large-scale stationary energy storage, esp. as a result of heightened interest in renewable energy sources that provide intermittent power which needs to be load-levelled.  The sodium-ion battery field presents many solid state materials design challenges, and rising to that call in the past couple of years, several reports of new sodium-ion technologies and electrode materials have surfaced.  These range from high-temp. air electrodes to new layered oxides, polyanion-based materials, carbons and other insertion materials for sodium-ion batteries, many of which hold promise for future sodium-based energy storage applications.  In this article, the challenges of current high-temp. sodium technologies including Na-S and Na-NiCl2 and new molten sodium technol., Na-O2 are summarized.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advanced Na[Ni0.25Fe0.5Mn0.25]O2/C-Fe3O4 Sodium-Ion Batteries Using EMS Electrolyte for Energy Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Oh, Seung-Min; Myung, Seung-Taek; Yoon, Chong Seung; Lu, Jun; Hassoun, Jusef; Scrosati, Bruno; Amine, Khalil; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">While much research effort was devoted to the development of advanced Li-ion batteries for renewal energy storage applications, the Na-ion battery is also of considerable interest because Na is one of the most abundant elements in the Earth's crust.  The authors report a Na-ion battery based on a C-coated Fe3O4 anode, Na[Ni0.25Fe0.5Mn0.25]O2 layered cathode, and NaClO4 in fluoroethylene carbonate and Et methanesulfonate electrolyte.  This unique battery system combines an intercalation cathode and a conversion anode, resulting in high capacity, high rate capability, thermal stability, and much improved cycle life.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors compare the interactions of lithium and sodium with a range of carbon materials in electrochem. cells.  Through wide angle in situ x-ray scattering studies, both lithium and sodium can be inserted into the interlayer space in disordered carbon materials.  This insertion process is accompanied by an increase in the interlayer spacing in these materials.  Small-angle in situ scattering studies are presented to clearly show the insertion of lithium and sodium into nanopores within disordered hard carbons.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical intercalation of sodium in graphite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ge, Pascal; Fouletier, Mireille</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Solid State Ionics</span>

J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ikuhara, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Why Is Sodium-intercalated Graphite Unstable?</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">36550</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">36554</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Why is sodium-intercalated graphite unstable?</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Moriwake, Hiroki; Kuwabara, Akihide; Fisher, Craig A. J.; Ikuhara, Yuichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">58</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">36550-36554</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">RSCACL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2046-2069</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries offer an attractive low-cost alternative to Li-ion batteries.  Although graphite is used as the neg. electrode in conventional Li-ion batteries, attempts to use it in Na-ion batteries have been hampered by the inability of Na to form graphite intercalation compds. (GICs) under moderate conditions.  It is generally considered that this is due to the size mismatch between Na and the graphite interlayer spacings, but here we show with detailed first-principles calcns. of Li, Na, K, Rb, and Cs GICs that the major reason is the change in chem. bonding between alkali metal (AM) ion and C atoms. van der Waals correction terms are introduced to better reproduce the layered graphite structure, and calcd. formation energies of GICs AMC6, where AM = Li, Na, K, Rb, and Cs, are found to become less neg. (less stable) as ion size decreases from Cs to Na as a result of weakening ionic bonding, until the formation energy of NaC6 becomes pos.  The much smaller Li ion represents an exception to this trend, as its bonds with C atoms contain a covalent component, resulting in a neg. formation energy.  These subtle differences in staging for the different alkali metals explain why graphite is a good intercalation material for Li and K but not for Na.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqpwjr7BwyyKbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgHaz7XpntUWg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXht1Whtr%252FP&amp;md5=a0de324fd0e2a07202566280f0456c52</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201701664"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wahid, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Puthusseri, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gawli, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sharma, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogale, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hard Carbons for Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes: Synthetic Strategies, Material Properties, and Storage Mechanisms</span>. <i>ChemSusChem</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">506</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">526</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are attracting much interest due to their potential as viable future alternatives for lithium-ion batteries, in view of the much higher earth abundance of sodium over that of lithium.  Although both battery systems have basically similar chemistries, the key celebrated neg. electrode in lithium battery, namely, graphite, is unavailable for the sodium-ion battery due to the larger size of the sodium ion.  This need is satisfied by "hard carbon", which can internalize the larger sodium ion and has desirable electrochem. properties.  Unlike graphite, with its specific layered structure, however, hard carbon occurs in diverse microstructural states.  Herein, the relationships between precursor choices, synthetic protocols, microstructural states, and performance features of hard carbon forms in the context of sodium-ion battery applications are elucidated.  Derived from the pertinent literature employing classical and modern structural characterization techniques, various issues related to microstructure, morphol., defects, and heteroatom doping are discussed.  Finally, an outlook is presented to suggest emerging research directions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp122kud8UHM7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgHaz7XpntUWg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhsl2gtb4%253D&amp;md5=1993d855710123c7f3e134a396dbe052</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201503816"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Banks, C.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanosized-bismuth-embedded 1D carbon nanofibers as high-performance anodes for lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yin, Hong; Li, Qingwei; Cao, Minglei; Zhang, Wei; Zhao, Han; Li, Chong; Huo, Kaifu; Zhu, Mingqiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Research</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Bi is a promising candidate for energy storage materials because of its high volumetric capacity, stability in moisture/air, and facile prepn.  In this study, the electrochem. performance of nanosized-Bi-embedded one-dimensional (1D) carbon nanofibers (Bi/C nanofibers) as anodes for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) and Na-ion batteries (NIBs) was systematically investigated.  The Bi/C nanofibers were prepd. using a single-nozzle electrospinning method with a specified Bi source followed by carbothermal redn.  Abundant Bi nanoparticles with diams. of approx. 20 nm were homogeneously dispersed and embedded in the 1D carbon nanofibers, as confirmed by structural and morphol. characterization.  Electrochem. measurements indicate that the Bi/C nanofiber anodes could deliver a long cycle life for LIBs and a preferable rate performance for NIBs.  The superior electrochem. performances of the Bi/C nanofiber anodes are attributed to the 1D carbon nanofiber structure and uniform distribution of Bi nanoparticles embedded in the carbon matrix.  This unique embedded structure provides a favorable electron carrier and buffering matrix for the effective release of mech. stress caused by vol. change and prevents the aggregation of Bi nanoparticles.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">S-doped porous carbon confined SnS nanospheres with enhanced electrochemical performance for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yaping; Zhang, Yifang; Shi, Junrong; Pan, Anqiang; Jiang, Feng; Liang, Shuquan; Cao, Guozhong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">37</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">18286-18292</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Stannous based anode materials have been extensively studied for sodium ion batteries due to their high theor. capacities.  However, the large vol. changes upon repeated cycling always cause their structural pulverization and capacity fading.  Herein, we report the fabrication of S-doped porous hollow carbon confined SnS nanospheres particles and their good electrochem. performance as an anode material for sodium ion batteries.  Unlike previous reports, stanniferous solid carbonaceous nanospheres are novelly prepd. by a hydrothermal process, and they can be converted into yolk-shell structured Sn@C nanospheres after thermal annealing in a H2/Ar atm.  The subsequent sulfidation process can produce S-doped carbon confined SnS hollow nanospheres with high porosity.  The obtained SnS@SPC nanospheres possess a large Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) surface area of 135.8 m2 g-1.  As an anode material for sodium ion batteries, the obtained yolk-shell SnS@SPC exhibits a high reversible capacity of 512 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 and good cycling stability.  The void space between the carbon shell and the SnS yolk can accommodate the vol. changes during the charge/discharge process.  Meanwhile, the porous carbon shell can serve as a conductive skeleton and a reservoir for the electrolyte.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrczDQvnYWhGbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liJJ3IqDWR4NA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhs1KgtLvI&amp;md5=4045cfd829235cafa3e6b59734ae9266</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1149/1.3607983"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chevrier, V.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been proposed as candidates for replacing Li-ion batteries.  The authors examine the viability of Na-ion anode materials based on Na alloys or hard carbons in terms of volumetric energy d.  Due to the bigger size of the Na atom compared to the Li atom, Na alloys would lead to anode materials with about half the volumetric energy d. of their Li analogs.  Volumetric energy densities obtainable with sodiated hard carbons would also be significantly less than those obtainable with lithiated graphite.  These findings highlight the need of novel ideas for Na-ion anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpfbyHmIC7uUrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liJJ3IqDWR4NA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXpt1Ont7c%253D&amp;md5=f132981922c6ce146def4cd54c721e2b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA10823K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Miao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structural design of anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Wanlin; Li, Weijie; Wang, Shun; Miao, Zongcheng; Liu, Hua Kun; Chou, Shulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">With the high consumption and increasing price of lithium resources, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as attractive and promising potential alternatives to lithium ion batteries, owing to the abundance and low cost of sodium resources, and the similar electrochem. properties of sodium to lithium.  Nevertheless, the lower energy d. and limited cycling life of SIBs are still the main challenges impeding their wide application.  Tremendous work has been done on anode materials for SIBs, and rational structural design is considered as an effective way to enhance their electrochem. performance.  In this review, different types of anode materials for SIBs are summarized according to their reaction mechanism, and the problems for each type are pointed out.  Specific structural design approaches for each type of anode material to improve its sodium storage performance are described in detail, and the benefits of different structural designs are explained as well.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpvuglkh65_0LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgVojcY_Gl-ZQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXit12mtr0%253D&amp;md5=c172db75a6e28ebb0bbbd82521fcaf6b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201702849"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advanced Phosphorus-Based Materials for Lithium/Sodium-Ion Batteries: Recent Developments and Future Perspectives</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Composite of graphite/phosphorus as anode for lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bai, Aojun; Wang, Li; Li, Jiaoyang; He, Xiangming; Wang, Jixian; Wang, Jianlong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite/Phosphorus composite anodes are prepd. by mixing graphite and the phosphorus/carbon material, which prepd. by heating the mixt. of red phosphorus and porous carbon.  Their electrochem. performances are evaluated as anodes for Li-ion batteries.  A graphite/Phosphorus composite|LiFePO4 full-cell is also attempted.  When the phosphorus/carbon content in the composite anode is 28.6 wt.%, the composite anode presents high reversible capacity of 500 mAh g-1 and considerable cyclability comparable to that of graphite anode, showing promising performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr0RUmZultS_7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgVojcY_Gl-ZQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXnsFGnu74%253D&amp;md5=b7a9fa72d547f06c01c3425edc07456e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06186A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ramireddy, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xing, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rahman, M. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>3</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5572</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5584</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C4TA06186A</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D3%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D5572-5584%26journal%3DJ.%2BMater.%2BChem.%2BA%26author%3DT.%2BRamireddy%26author%3DT.%2BXing%26author%3DM.%2BM.%2BRahman%26author%3DY.%2BChen%26author%3DQ.%2BDutercq%26author%3DD.%2BGunzelmann%26author%3DA.%2BM.%2BGlushenkov%26title%3DPhosphorus%25E2%2580%2593carbon%2BNanocomposite%2BAnodes%2Bfor%2BLithium-ion%2Band%2BSodium-ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC4TA06186A&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.energyfuels.9b00385&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC4TA06186A&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Phosphorus-carbon nanocomposite anodes for lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ramireddy, Thrinathreddy; Xing, Tan; Rahman, Md Mokhlesur; Chen, Ying; Dutercq, Quentin; Gunzelmann, Daniel; Glushenkov, Alexey M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5572-5584</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">With the expected theor. capacity of 2596 mA h g-1, P is considered to be the highest capacity anode material for Na-ion batteries and one of the most attractive anode materials for Li-ion systems.  This work presents a comprehensive study of P-C nanocomposite anodes for both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries.  The composite electrodes are able to display high initial capacities of ∼1700 and 1300 mA h g-1 in Li and Na half-cells, resp., when the cells are tested within a larger potential windows of 2.0-0.01 V vs. Li/Li+ and Na/Na+.  The level of demonstrated capacity is underpinned by the storage mechanism, based on the transformation of P to Li3P phase for Li cells and an incomplete transformation to Na3P phase for Na cells.  The capacity deteriorates upon cycling, which is shown to originate from disintegration of electrodes and their delamination from current collectors by post-cycling ex situ electron microscopy.  Stable cyclic performance at the level of ∼700 and ∼350-400 mA h g-1 can be achieved if the potential windows are restricted to 2.0-0.67 V vs. Li/Li+ for Li and 2-0.33 vs. Na/Na+ for Na half-cells.  The results are critically discussed in light of literature reports.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpbpTdmNtgwtrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgVojcY_Gl-ZQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXit1aqt7o%253D&amp;md5=964498820e44c8f37b2e4e369def7076</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1002/ange.201209689"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Capacity and Rate Capability of Amorphous Phosphorus for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Angew.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High performance red phosphorus electrode in ionic liquid-based electrolyte for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dahbi, Mouad; Fukunishi, Mika; Horiba, Tatsuo; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Yasuno, Satoshi; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. performance of the red P electrode was examd. in ionic-liq. electrolyte, 0.25 mol dm-3 Na bisfluorosulfonylamide (NaFSA) dissolved N-methyl-N-propyl-pyridinium-bisfluorosulfonylamide (MPPFSA), at room temp.  The authors compared its electrochem. performance to conventional EC/PC/DEC, EC/DEC, and PC solns. contg. 1 mol dm-3 NaPF6.  The electrode in NaFSA/MPPFSA demonstrated a reversible capacity of 1480 mA h g-1 and excellent capacity retention of 93% over 80 cycles, which is much better than those in the conventional electrolytes.  The difference in capacity retention for the electrolytes correlates to the different solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer formed on the P electrode.  To understand the SEI formation in NaFSA/MPPFSA and its evolution during cycling, the authors study the surface layer of the red P electrodes with hard XPS (HAXPES) and time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS).  A detailed anal. of HAXPES spectra demonstrates that SEI layer consists of major inorg. and minor org. species, originating from decompn. of MPP+ and FSA-.  Homogeneous surface layer is formed during the 1st cycle in NaFSA/MPPFSA while in alkyl carbonate ester electrolytes, continuous growth of surface film up to the 20th cycle is obsd.  Possibility of red phosphorous electrode for battery applications with pure ionic liq. is discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrxFJ3F7Y7_n7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgVojcY_Gl-ZQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXht1yhtrvJ&amp;md5=b1c4e457edecba628f8d455264d5e940</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA10668H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, S.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High energy density rechargeable metal-free seawater batteries: a phosphorus/carbon composite as a promising anode material</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Yongil; Hwang, Soo Min; Yu, Hyein; Kim, Youngsik</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A new energy conversion and storage system, named the 'seawater battery', has recently been a subject of research in the field of electrochem.  Using natural seawater as the catholyte in an open-structured cathode, the battery stores sodium (Na) ions from the seawater on the anode side during charging, and then delivers electricity by discharging on demand.  Herein, we report an amorphous red phosphorus/carbon composite anode material which was successfully employed as the anode of a seawater battery.  It exhibited a stable cycling performance with a high reversible capacity exceeding 920 mA h gcomposite-1 with a coulombic efficiency of more than 92% over 80 cycles, as well as good rate capabilities.  In terms of the full-cell performance of lithium ion and sodium ion batteries, the seawater batteries with the amorphous red phosphorus/carbon composite anode exhibited the highest reversible capacity and specific energy.  These results indicate that the use of an open-structured cathode system, which provides the anode with an unlimited supply of Na ions, would allow a seawater battery to overcome the limitations assocd. with the high-capacity alloying reaction-based anodes used in conventional batteries with a closed system.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq3_I7Hew-RH7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgFU3Bjveqn8Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXnvVKlsw%253D%253D&amp;md5=c833a64b9f2a1cfc9c9351c5612b9c5f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2017.12.077"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qi, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xing, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhuang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ju, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enhanced Capacity of Chemically Bonded Phosphorus/carbon Composite as an Anode Material for Potassium-ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Potassium-ion batteries are attracting great attention as a promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries due to the abundance and low price of potassium.                         cas_cit37R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">37</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Formation of stable phosphorus-carbon bond for enhanced performance in black phosphorus nanoparticle-graphite composite battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Jie; Zheng, Guangyuan; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Liu, Nian; Wang, Haotian; Yao, Hongbin; Yang, Wensheng; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High specific capacity battery electrode materials have attracted great research attention.  Phosphorus as a low-cost abundant material has a high theor. specific capacity of 2596 mAh/g with most of its capacity at the discharge potential range of 0.4-1.2 V, suitable as anodes.  Although numerous research progress have shown other high capacity anodes such as Si, Ge, Sn, and SnO2, there are only a few studies on phosphorus anodes despite its high theor. capacity.  Successful applications of phosphorus anodes have been impeded by rapid capacity fading, mainly caused by large vol. change (around 300%) upon lithiation and thus loss of elec. contact.  Using the conducting allotrope of phosphorus, "black phosphorus" as starting materials, here we fabricated composites of black phosphorus nanoparticle-graphite by mechanochem. reaction in a high energy mech. milling process.  This process produces phosphorus-carbon bonds, which are stable during lithium insertion/extn., maintaining excellent elec. connection between phosphorus and carbon.  We demonstrated high initial discharge capacity of 2786 mAh·g-1 at 0.2 C and an excellent cycle life of 100 cycles with 80% capacity retention.  High specific discharge capacities are maintained at fast C rates (2270, 1750, 1500, and 1240 mAh·g-1 at C/5, 1, 2, and 4.5 C, resp.).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrF9QgJP-i-_7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgFU3Bjveqn8Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtFKlsrnK&amp;md5=c6104bd8b2513fddbe4b116491da865f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1007/s10570-014-0264-9"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kuga, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Exfoliation of Graphite by Dry Ball Milling with Cellulose</span>. <i>Cellulose</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>21</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2469</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2478</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exfoliation of graphite by dry ball milling with cellulose</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Peipei; Kuga, Shigenori; Wu, Min; Huang, Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Cellulose (Dordrecht, Netherlands)</span>

                        cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Phosphorus electrodes in sodium cells: small volume expansion by sodiation and the surface-stabilization mechanism in aprotic solvent</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Matsuura, Yuta; Ishikawa, Toru; Kuze, Satoru; Son, Jin-Young; Cui, Yi-Tao; Oji, Hiroshi; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemElectroChem</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrode performance of amorphous phosphorus in aprotic Na cells is examd.  Amorphous phosphorus is electrochem. reduced in the Na cells with a three-electron redox process, crystg. into Na3P.  Na-P bonds in Na3P have high covalent characteristics.  Therefore, the molar volume of Na in Na3P is anomalously small in comparison to other Na-metal alloys that have been used as neg. electrode materials.  The theor. volumetric capacity, calcd. at full vol. expansion of amorphous phosphorus electrodes through sodiation, is expected to be 27% larger than that of metallic sodium.  However, exptl., it is found that severe electrolyte decompn. results in insufficient reversibility of the electrode materials, owing to the highly reactive Na3P surface.  Electrode reversibility is successfully improved by utilizing an electrolyte additive, fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC).  The surface chem. of phosphorus in the aprotic solvent with sodium salts is examd. by hard/soft XPS (PES).  PES studies reveal that FEC effectively stabilizes the solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI), contg. monovalent phosphorus species and sodium fluoride, and thus electrolyte decompn. is partly suppressed by the relatively stable SEI formed on the surface of phosphorus particles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqpBUNr-VIkQrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgFU3Bjveqn8Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhslWisrvK&amp;md5=ed8f5cf0ca24d4e057ca230d2c04e29c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit40" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.7b01128"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Marino, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">El Kazzi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Berg, E.                 10.1021/acs.chemmater.7b01128</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D29%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D7151-7158%26journal%3DChem.%2BMater.%26author%3DC.%2BMarino%26author%3DM.%2BEl%2BKazzi%26author%3DE.%2BJ.%2BBerg%26author%3DM.%2BHe%26author%3DC.%2BVillevieille%26title%3DInterface%2Band%2BSafety%2BProperties%2Bof%2BPhosphorus-Based%2BNegative%2BElectrodes%2Bin%2BLi-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.chemmater.7b01128&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.energyfuels.9b00385&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.7b01128&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interface and Safety Properties of Phosphorus-Based Negative Electrodes in Li-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Marino, C.; El Kazzi, M.; Berg, E. J.; He, M.; Villevieille, C.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">29</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7151-7158</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Phosphorus is considered as a promising candidate for the replacement of graphite as the active material in Li-ion battery electrodes owing to its 6-fold higher theor. specific charge.  Unfortunately, phosphorus-based electrodes suffer from large vol. changes upon cycling, leading to poor electrochem. performance.  Furthermore, red phosphorus (Pred) is known to release phosphine gas (PH3) once in contact with water (even at the ppm level), and thus, its safety profile needs to be assessed.  In this context, the electrolyte/electrode interface of a Pred electrode during the first lithiation is fully investigated using SEM (SEM), XPS, and online electrochem. mass spectroscopy (OEMS).  The XPS analyses reveal that, at potentials higher than 1 V vs Li+/Li, the Pred starts to react via the outermost surface layer, which is mainly composed of the native oxide, P2O5, to form H3PO4.  Once this surface oxide is consumed, the Pred reacts with moisture and the electrolyte, resulting in the re-formation of H3PO4 and the release of the toxic PH3 as identified by OEMS.  At potential lower than 1 V, a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) develops on the top of H3PO4 as identified by XPS analyses.  This SEI prevents further degrdn. of the Pred and inhibits PH3 release.  Following the lithiation, the reaction of Li with Pred generates particle fracture (identified by SEM) and the transformation of H3PO4 into Li3PO4 is also noticed.  Understanding and monitoring the role of the decompn. products and processes within the battery is crucial to further improving battery performance and safety.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoqeZozqhDPybVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgdV7AgBYsqSQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtlWnsrrP&amp;md5=0a1a3d21f8d578edb6ffe6dce4781004</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1038/s41598-017-06555-8"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Veith, G. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Doucet, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sacci, R. L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vacaliuc, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Baldwin, J. K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Browning, J. F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Determination of the Solid Electrolyte Interphase Structure Grown on a Silicon Electrode Using a Fluoroethylene Carbonate Additive</span>. <i>Sci.                 10.1038/s41598-017-06555-8</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D7%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D6326%26journal%3DSci.%2BRep.%26author%3DG.%2BM.%2BVeith%26author%3DM.%2BDoucet%26author%3DR.%2BL.%2BSacci%26author%3DB.%2BVacaliuc%26author%3DJ.%2BK.%2BBaldwin%26author%3DJ.%2BF.%2BBrowning%26title%3DDetermination%2Bof%2Bthe%2BSolid%2BElectrolyte%2BInterphase%2BStructure%2BGrown%2Bon%2Ba%2BSilicon%2BElectrode%2BUsing%2Ba%2BFluoroethylene%2BCarbonate%2BAdditive%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fs41598-017-06555-8&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.energyfuels.9b00385&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fs41598-017-06555-8&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Determination of the Solid Electrolyte Interphase Structure Grown on a Silicon Electrode Using a Fluoroethylene Carbonate Additive</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Veith Gabriel M; Sacci Robert L; Doucet Mathieu; Vacaliuc Bogdan; Baldwin J Kevin; Browning James F</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Scientific reports</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6326</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work we explore how an electrolyte additive (fluorinated ethylene carbonate - FEC) mediates the thickness and composition of the solid electrolyte interphase formed over a silicon anode in situ as a function of state-of-charge and cycle.  We show the FEC condenses on the surface at open circuit voltage then is reduced to C-O containing polymeric species around 0.9 V (vs.  Li/Li(+)).  The resulting film is about 50 ÅA thick.  Upon lithiation the SEI thickens to 70 ÅA and becomes more organic-like.  With delithiation the SEI thins by 13 ÅA and becomes more inorganic in nature, consistent with the formation of LiF.  This thickening/thinning is reversible with cycling and shows the SEI is a dynamic structure.  We compare the SEI chemistry and thickness to 280 ÅA thick SEI layers produced without FEC and provide a mechanism for SEI formation using FEC additives.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRg1LVxpT6ystFxYDaSSP8XfW6udTcc2ebt6qE67HG0_bntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1cfgslyrtg%253D%253D&amp;md5=939d5f698542f659160eca3d643d2c98</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Walz, R.</span></span> <i>Chemical Behaviour of Red Phosphorus in Water</i>; <span class="NLM_publisher-name">Clariant</span>: <span class="NLM_publisher-loc">Sulzbach, Germany</span>, <span class="NLM_year">2000</span>.</span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?hl=en&amp;publication_year=2000&amp;author=R.+Walz&amp;title=Chemical+Behaviour+of+Red+Phosphorus+in+Water" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="

Shown Here Is a Schematic of the Division of the Milling Procedure for Preparing the RP–Carbon Composite<sup>a</sup></div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/large/ef-2019-00385d_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div><p class="last"></p><p class="last"><span class="fn-label"><sup>a</sup></span>In the first step (gold arrows), commercial RP is wet-ball-milled in EG for time <i>t</i><sub>1</sub> to reduce the particle size, which mitigates pulverization on sodiation. Milling the RP alone allows for the accurate measurement of its particle-size distribution before formation of the composite. In the second step (silver arrows), the RP from step one is combined with graphite in a 7:3 ratio and dry-ball-milled for time <i>t</i><sub>2</sub> to form an electronically conductive RP–carbon composite suitable for use as an anode.</p><p></p></figure><figure data-id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/medium/ef-2019-00385d_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/large/ef-2019-00385d_0001.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr2" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Particle-size distributions of the ball-milled RP shift left (to smaller primary particle sizes) relative to the commercial RP with increasing milling time. After 12 h, the particle-size distribution moves to a smaller size, with the mode of about 0.6 μm. After 18 h, the mode is almost unchanged (0.55 μm), but most particles in the range of 2–10 μm have been broken apart. After 60 h, the particle-size distribution further shifts to a smaller particle size, showing a mode of about 0.30 μm. (b) Dv90, as determined by DLS, falls rapidly as a function of the milling time in the EG solvent.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/large/ef-2019-00385d_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/medium/ef-2019-00385d_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/large/ef-2019-00385d_0002.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr3" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Composites were galvanostatically cycled at C/15, and the curves for cycles 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, 50, and 100 of the composites prepared from different Dv90 of (a) 1.85 μm, (b) 1.26 μm, and (c) 0.79 μm are shown. (d) For each of the three composites in panels a–c, the capacities at C/15 for both sodiation (open markers) and desodiation (closed markers) are plotted. The sodiation and desodiation capacities are closely overlaid because of their very high columbic efficiencies, plotted in panel e. (f) Evolution of the capacities for both sodiation (open markers) and desodiation (closed markers) is plotted along with the coulombic efficiency for the three composites at different C rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/large/ef-2019-00385d_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/medium/ef-2019-00385d_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/large/ef-2019-00385d_0003.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr4" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Composites were galvanostatically cycled at C/15, and the curves for cycles 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, 50, and 100 are shown for various composite milling times: (a) 1 h, (b) 24 h, and (c) 48 h. (d) For the 24 and 48 h milling-time composites in panels b and c, the capacities at C/15 for both sodiation (open markers) and desodiation (closed markers) are plotted. The sodiation and desodiation capacities are closely overlaid because of their very high columbic efficiencies, plotted in panel e. (f) Evolution of the capacities for both sodiation (open markers) and desodiation (closed markers) is plotted along with the coulombic efficiency for the three composites at different C rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/large/ef-2019-00385d_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/medium/ef-2019-00385d_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/large/ef-2019-00385d_0004.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr5" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Cross sections of electrodes were prepared via a precision etching and coating system (PECS). The three images are of the composites ball milled for (a) 1 h, (b) 24 h, and (c) 48 h. Both the SEM and EDS data show that, with increasing milling time, the composite becomes more spatially uniform.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/large/ef-2019-00385d_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig5"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/medium/ef-2019-00385d_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/large/ef-2019-00385d_0005.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr6" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) TEM image of the composite material made by mixing phosphorus (Dv90 = 0.79 μm) with graphite for 48 h in which graphene planes can be seen on the surface of the phosphorus particle. (b) Plotting the ratio between the integrated areas of the peaks fitted on the photoelectron spectra collected from the composite versus the probing depth shows that surficial P–C chemical bonds gradually decrease and P–P bonds increase as we move deeper toward the particle bulk. The areas are calculated from the fit shown in panels c–e, with the photoelectron spectra of the P 2p region acquired using increasing incident radiation energy.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385/asset/images/large/ef-2019-00385d_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.energyfuels.9b00385&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></li><li id="pane-m2" aria-labelledby="pane-m2" class="tab__pane tab__pane-references"><div class="refs-header-label"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i15">References</h2></div></div></div><hr></div><p class="rightTab-references-count">

Our use of energy in the twenty-first century must also be sustainable. This Perspective puts these opportunities into a larger context by relating them to a no. of aspects in the transportation and electricity generation sectors.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  In recent years, with the deployment of renewable energy sources, advances in electrified transportation, and development in smart grids, the markets for large-scale stationary energy storage have grown rapidly.  Electrochem. energy storage methods are strong candidate solns. due to their high energy d., flexibility, and scalability.  This review provides an overview of mature and emerging technologies for secondary and redox flow batteries.  New developments in the chem. of secondary and flow batteries as well as regenerative fuel cells are also considered.  Advantages and disadvantages of current and prospective electrochem. energy storage options are discussed.  The most promising technologies in the short term are high-temp. sodium batteries with β"-alumina electrolyte, lithium-ion batteries, and flow batteries.                         rightTab-cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgQBop0L7PD_w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1016/j.cossms.2012.04.002"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ellis, B. Not only will that continue, but they are also now powering plug-in hybrid elec. vehicles and elec. vehicles.  In light of possible concerns over rising lithium costs in the future, Na and Na-ion batteries have re-emerged as candidates for medium and large-scale stationary energy storage, esp. as a result of heightened interest in renewable energy sources that provide intermittent power which needs to be load-levelled.  The sodium-ion battery field presents many solid state materials design challenges, and rising to that call in the past couple of years, several reports of new sodium-ion technologies and electrode materials have surfaced.  These range from high-temp. air electrodes to new layered oxides, polyanion-based materials, carbons and other insertion materials for sodium-ion batteries, many of which hold promise for future sodium-based energy storage applications.  In this article, the challenges of current high-temp. sodium technologies including Na-S and Na-NiCl2 and new molten sodium technol., Na-O2 are summarized. J.; Ikuhara, Yuichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">58</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries offer an attractive low-cost alternative to Li-ion batteries.  Although graphite is used as the neg. electrode in conventional Li-ion batteries, attempts to use it in Na-ion batteries have been hampered by the inability of Na to form graphite intercalation compds. (GICs) under moderate conditions.  It is generally considered that this is due to the size mismatch between Na and the graphite interlayer spacings, but here we show with detailed first-principles calcns. of Li, Na, K, Rb, and Cs GICs that the major reason is the change in chem. bonding between alkali metal (AM) ion and C atoms. van der Waals correction terms are introduced to better reproduce the layered graphite structure, and calcd. formation energies of GICs AMC6, where AM = Li, Na, K, Rb, and Cs, are found to become less neg. (less stable) as ion size decreases from Cs to Na as a result of weakening ionic bonding, until the formation energy of NaC6 becomes pos.  The much smaller Li ion represents an exception to this trend, as its bonds with C atoms contain a covalent component, resulting in a neg. formation energy.  These subtle differences in staging for the different alkali metals explain why graphite is a good intercalation material for Li and K but not for Na.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqpwjr7BwyyKbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljME3EWTBj58Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXht1Whtr%252FP&amp;md5=a0de324fd0e2a07202566280f0456c52</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201701664"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wahid, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Puthusseri, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gawli, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sharma, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogale, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hard Carbons for Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes: Synthetic Strategies, Material Properties, and Storage Mechanisms</span>. <i>ChemSusChem</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">506</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">526</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are attracting much interest due to their potential as viable future alternatives for lithium-ion batteries, in view of the much higher earth abundance of sodium over that of lithium.  Although both battery systems have basically similar chemistries, the key celebrated neg. electrode in lithium battery, namely, graphite, is unavailable for the sodium-ion battery due to the larger size of the sodium ion.  This need is satisfied by "hard carbon", which can internalize the larger sodium ion and has desirable electrochem. properties.  Unlike graphite, with its specific layered structure, however, hard carbon occurs in diverse microstructural states.  Herein, the relationships between precursor choices, synthetic protocols, microstructural states, and performance features of hard carbon forms in the context of sodium-ion battery applications are elucidated.  Derived from the pertinent literature employing classical and modern structural characterization techniques, various issues related to microstructure, morphol., defects, and heteroatom doping are discussed.  Finally, an outlook is presented to suggest emerging research directions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp122kud8UHM7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljME3EWTBj58Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhsl2gtb4%253D&amp;md5=1993d855710123c7f3e134a396dbe052</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref19" class="rightTabRefNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit19" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201503816"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Banks, C. The superior electrochem. performances of the Bi/C nanofiber anodes are attributed to the 1D carbon nanofiber structure and uniform distribution of Bi nanoparticles embedded in the carbon matrix.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Stannous based anode materials have been extensively studied for sodium ion batteries due to their high theor. capacities.  However, the large vol. changes upon repeated cycling always cause their structural pulverization and capacity fading.  Herein, we report the fabrication of S-doped porous hollow carbon confined SnS nanospheres particles and their good electrochem. performance as an anode material for sodium ion batteries.  Unlike previous reports, stanniferous solid carbonaceous nanospheres are novelly prepd. by a hydrothermal process, and they can be converted into yolk-shell structured Sn@C nanospheres after thermal annealing in a H2/Ar atm.  The subsequent sulfidation process can produce S-doped carbon confined SnS hollow nanospheres with high porosity.  The obtained SnS@SPC nanospheres possess a large Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) surface area of 135.8 m2 g-1.  As an anode material for sodium ion batteries, the obtained yolk-shell SnS@SPC exhibits a high reversible capacity of 512 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 and good cycling stability.  The void space between the carbon shell and the SnS yolk can accommodate the vol. changes during the charge/discharge process.  Meanwhile, the porous carbon shell can serve as a conductive skeleton and a reservoir for the electrolyte.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrczDQvnYWhGbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhxD4Joirv1lQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhs1KgtLvI&amp;md5=4045cfd829235cafa3e6b59734ae9266</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1149/1.3607983"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chevrier, V.         </div><div class="casAbstract">With the high consumption and increasing price of lithium resources, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as attractive and promising potential alternatives to lithium ion batteries, owing to the abundance and low cost of sodium resources, and the similar electrochem. properties of sodium to lithium.  Nevertheless, the lower energy d. and limited cycling life of SIBs are still the main challenges impeding their wide application.  Tremendous work has been done on anode materials for SIBs, and rational structural design is considered as an effective way to enhance their electrochem. performance.  In this review, different types of anode materials for SIBs are summarized according to their reaction mechanism, and the problems for each type are pointed out.  Specific structural design approaches for each type of anode material to improve its sodium storage performance are described in detail, and the benefits of different structural designs are explained as well.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpvuglkh65_0LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLNcreELOcsQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXit12mtr0%253D&amp;md5=c172db75a6e28ebb0bbbd82521fcaf6b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref26" class="rightTabRefNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit26" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201702849"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advanced Phosphorus-Based Materials for Lithium/Sodium-Ion Batteries: Recent Developments and Future Perspectives</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">With the expected theor. capacity of 2596 mA h g-1, P is considered to be the highest capacity anode material for Na-ion batteries and one of the most attractive anode materials for Li-ion systems.  This work presents a comprehensive study of P-C nanocomposite anodes for both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries.  The composite electrodes are able to display high initial capacities of ∼1700 and 1300 mA h g-1 in Li and Na half-cells, resp., when the cells are tested within a larger potential windows of 2.0-0.01 V vs. Li/Li+ and Na/Na+.  The level of demonstrated capacity is underpinned by the storage mechanism, based on the transformation of P to Li3P phase for Li cells and an incomplete transformation to Na3P phase for Na cells.  The capacity deteriorates upon cycling, which is shown to originate from disintegration of electrodes and their delamination from current collectors by post-cycling ex situ electron microscopy.  Stable cyclic performance at the level of ∼700 and ∼350-400 mA h g-1 can be achieved if the potential windows are restricted to 2.0-0.67 V vs. Li/Li+ for Li and 2-0.33 vs. Na/Na+ for Na half-cells.  The results are critically discussed in light of literature reports.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpbpTdmNtgwtrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLNcreELOcsQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXit1aqt7o%253D&amp;md5=964498820e44c8f37b2e4e369def7076</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref31" class="rightTabRefNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit31" data-doi="10.1002/ange.201209689"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Capacity and Rate Capability of Amorphous Phosphorus for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Angew.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. performance of the red P electrode was examd. in ionic-liq. electrolyte, 0.25 mol dm-3 Na bisfluorosulfonylamide (NaFSA) dissolved N-methyl-N-propyl-pyridinium-bisfluorosulfonylamide (MPPFSA), at room temp.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A new energy conversion and storage system, named the 'seawater battery', has recently been a subject of research in the field of electrochem.  Using natural seawater as the catholyte in an open-structured cathode, the battery stores sodium (Na) ions from the seawater on the anode side during charging, and then delivers electricity by discharging on demand.  Herein, we report an amorphous red phosphorus/carbon composite anode material which was successfully employed as the anode of a seawater battery.  It exhibited a stable cycling performance with a high reversible capacity exceeding 920 mA h gcomposite-1 with a coulombic efficiency of more than 92% over 80 cycles, as well as good rate capabilities.  In terms of the full-cell performance of lithium ion and sodium ion batteries, the seawater batteries with the amorphous red phosphorus/carbon composite anode exhibited the highest reversible capacity and specific energy. Although numerous research progress have shown other high capacity anodes such as Si, Ge, Sn, and SnO2, there are only a few studies on phosphorus anodes despite its high theor. capacity.
